</div>
			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Algae fuel</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Algae_fuel_in_a_beaker.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Algae_fuel_in_a_beaker.jpg/220px-Algae_fuel_in_a_beaker.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Algae_fuel_in_a_beaker.jpg/330px-Algae_fuel_in_a_beaker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Algae_fuel_in_a_beaker.jpg/440px-Algae_fuel_in_a_beaker.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1097" data-file-height="702" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Algae_fuel_in_a_beaker.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A conical flask of "green" <a href="/wiki/Jet_fuel" title="Jet fuel">jet fuel</a> made from algae</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Algae fuel</b>, <b>algal biofuel</b>, or <b>algal oil</b> is an <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel" title="Alternative fuel">alternative to liquid fossil fuels</a> that uses <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> as its source of energy-rich oils. Also, algae fuels are an alternative to common known biofuel sources, such as corn and sugarcane.<sup id="cite_ref-doi10.1016.2Fj.copbio.2010.03.005_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1016.2Fj.copbio.2010.03.005-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Several companies and government agencies are funding efforts to reduce capital and operating costs and make algae fuel production commercially viable.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Like fossil fuel, algae fuel releases CO<sub>2</sub> when burnt, but unlike fossil fuel, algae fuel and other biofuels only release CO<sub>2</sub> recently removed from the atmosphere via photosynthesis as the algae or plant grew. The energy crisis and the <a href="/wiki/World_food_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="World food crisis">world food crisis</a> have ignited interest in <a href="/wiki/Algaculture" title="Algaculture">algaculture</a> (farming algae) for making <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a> using land unsuitable for agriculture. Among algal fuels' attractive characteristics are that they can be grown with minimal impact on <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> resources,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gas2.0_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gas2.0-5">[5]</a></sup> can be produced using saline and <a href="/wiki/Wastewater" title="Wastewater">wastewater</a>, have a high <a href="/wiki/Flash_point" title="Flash point">flash point</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and are <a href="/wiki/Biodegradable" class="mw-redirect" title="Biodegradable">biodegradable</a> and relatively harmless to the environment if spilled.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Algae cost more per unit mass than other second-generation biofuel crops due to high capital and operating costs,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> but are claimed to yield between 10 and 100 times more fuel per unit area.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">United States Department of Energy</a> estimates that if algae fuel replaced all the petroleum fuel in the United States, it would require 15,000 square miles (39,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup>), which is only 0.42% of the U.S. map,<sup id="cite_ref-Hartman_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hartman-11">[11]</a></sup> or about half of the land area of <a href="/wiki/Maine" title="Maine">Maine</a>. This is less than <span class="nowrap"> </span><span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>⁄<sub>7</sub></span> the area of <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">corn</a> harvested in the United States in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the head of the Algal Biomass Organization, algae fuel can reach price parity with oil in 2018 if granted production <a href="/wiki/Tax_credits" class="mw-redirect" title="Tax credits">tax credits</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-parity_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-parity-13">[13]</a></sup> However, in 2013, <a href="/wiki/Exxon_Mobil" class="mw-redirect" title="Exxon Mobil">Exxon Mobil</a> Chairman and CEO <a href="/wiki/Rex_Tillerson" title="Rex Tillerson">Rex Tillerson</a> said that after committing to spend up to $600 million over 10 years on development in a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/J._Craig_Venter" class="mw-redirect" title="J. Craig Venter">J. Craig Venter</a>’s <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_Genomics" title="Synthetic Genomics">Synthetic Genomics</a> in 2009, Exxon pulled back after four years (and $100 million) when it realized that algae fuel is "probably further" than 25 years away from commercial viability.<sup id="cite_ref-Tillerson_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tillerson-14">[14]</a></sup> On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/Solazyme" class="mw-redirect" title="Solazyme">Solazyme</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Erin_Voegele_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Erin_Voegele-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sapphire_Energy" title="Sapphire Energy">Sapphire Energy</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Andrew_Herndon_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andrew_Herndon-16">[16]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Algenol" title="Algenol">Algenol</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> among others have begun commercial sale of algal biofuel in 2012 and 2013, and 2015, respectively.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1942 Harder and Von Witsch were the first to propose that <a href="/wiki/Microalgae" class="mw-redirect" title="Microalgae">microalgae</a> be grown as a source of lipids for food or fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Following World War II, research began in the US,<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Germany,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Japan,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> England,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> and Israel<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> on culturing techniques and engineering systems for growing microalgae on larger scales, particularly species in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Chlorella" title="Chlorella">Chlorella</a></i>. Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Hans_G%C3%BCnther_Aach" title="Hans Günther Aach">H. G. Aach</a> showed that <i><a href="/wiki/Chlorella_pyrenoidosa" title="Chlorella pyrenoidosa">Chlorella pyrenoidosa</a></i> could be induced via nitrogen starvation to accumulate as much as 70% of its dry weight as lipids.<sup id="cite_ref-doi10.1007.2FBF00410827_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1007.2FBF00410827-27">[27]</a></sup> Since the need for alternative transportation fuel had subsided after World War II, research at this time focused on culturing algae as a food source or, in some cases, for wastewater treatment.<sup id="cite_ref-doi10.1007.2F978-94-007-5479-9_1_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1007.2F978-94-007-5479-9_1-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Interest in the application of algae for biofuels was rekindled during the oil embargo and oil price surges of the 1970s, leading the US Department of Energy to initiate the <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_Species_Program" title="Aquatic Species Program">Aquatic Species Program</a> in 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-AlgalBiofuelsRoadmap_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AlgalBiofuelsRoadmap-29">[29]</a></sup> The Aquatic Species Program spent $25 million over 18 years with the goal of developing liquid transportation fuel from algae that would be price competitive with petroleum-derived fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1-30">[30]</a></sup> The research program focused on the cultivation of microalgae in open outdoor ponds, systems which are low in cost but vulnerable to environmental disturbances like temperature swings and biological invasions. 3,000 algal strains were collected from around the country and screened for desirable properties such as high productivity, lipid content, and thermal tolerance, and the most promising strains were included in the SERI microalgae collection at the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory#History" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">Solar Energy Research Institute</a> (SERI) in Golden, Colorado and used for further research.<sup id="cite_ref-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1-30">[30]</a></sup> Among the program’s most significant findings were that rapid growth and high lipid production were "mutually exclusive," since the former required high nutrients and the latter required low nutrients.<sup id="cite_ref-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1-30">[30]</a></sup> The final report suggested that <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> may be necessary to be able to overcome this and other natural limitations of algal strains, and that the ideal species might vary with place and season.<sup id="cite_ref-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1_30-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1-30">[30]</a></sup> Although it was successfully demonstrated that large-scale production of algae for fuel in outdoor ponds was feasible, the program failed to do so at a cost that would be competitive with petroleum, especially as oil prices sank in the 1990s. Even in the best case scenario, it was estimated that unextracted algal oil would cost $59–186 per barrel,<sup id="cite_ref-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1_30-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1-30">[30]</a></sup> while petroleum cost less than $20 per barrel in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-AlgalBiofuelsRoadmap_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AlgalBiofuelsRoadmap-29">[29]</a></sup> Therefore, under budget pressure in 1996, the Aquatic Species Program was abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1_30-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other contributions to algal biofuels research have come indirectly from projects focusing on different applications of algal cultures. For example, in the 1990s Japan’s Research Institute of Innovative Technology for the Earth (RITE) implemented a research program with the goal of developing systems to fix <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> using microalgae.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Although the goal was not energy production, several studies produced by RITE demonstrated that algae could be grown using flue gas from power plants as a <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> source,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> an important development for algal biofuel research. Other work focusing on harvesting hydrogen gas, methane, or ethanol from algae, as well as nutritional supplements and pharmaceutical compounds, has also helped inform research on biofuel production from algae.<sup id="cite_ref-doi10.1007.2F978-94-007-5479-9_1_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1007.2F978-94-007-5479-9_1-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the disbanding of the Aquatic Species Program in 1996, there was a relative lull in algal biofuel research. Still, various projects were funded in the US by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">Department of Energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Defense" title="United States Department of Defense">Department of Defense</a>, <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Agriculture" title="United States Department of Agriculture">Department of Agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy_national_laboratories" title="United States Department of Energy national laboratories">National Laboratories</a>, state funding, and private funding, as well as in other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-AlgalBiofuelsRoadmap_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AlgalBiofuelsRoadmap-29">[29]</a></sup> More recently, rising oil prices in the 2000s spurred a revival of interest in algal biofuels and US federal funding has increased,<sup id="cite_ref-AlgalBiofuelsRoadmap_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AlgalBiofuelsRoadmap-29">[29]</a></sup> numerous research projects are being funded in Australia, New Zealand, Europe, the Middle East, and other parts of the world,<sup id="cite_ref-Pienko.2C_P.T._2009_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pienko.2C_P.T._2009-34">[34]</a></sup> and a wave of private companies has entered the field<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> (see <a href="/wiki/Algae_fuel#Companies" title="Algae fuel">Companies</a>). In November 2012, <a href="/wiki/Solazyme" class="mw-redirect" title="Solazyme">Solazyme</a> and Propel Fuels made the first retail sales of algae-derived fuel,<sup id="cite_ref-Erin_Voegele_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Erin_Voegele-15">[15]</a></sup> and in March 2013 <a href="/wiki/Sapphire_Energy" title="Sapphire Energy">Sapphire Energy</a> began commercial sales of algal biofuel to <a href="/wiki/Tesoro" title="Tesoro">Tesoro</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Andrew_Herndon_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andrew_Herndon-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Food_supplementation">Food supplementation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Food supplementation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Algal oil is used as a source of <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acid</a> supplementation in food products, particularly <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a>, as it is heavy in <a href="/wiki/Monounsaturated_fat" title="Monounsaturated fat">mono-</a> and <a href="/wiki/Polyunsaturated_fat" title="Polyunsaturated fat">polyunsaturated fats</a>, in particular <a href="/wiki/Eicosapentaenoic_acid" title="Eicosapentaenoic acid">EPA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Docosahexaenoic_acid" title="Docosahexaenoic acid">DHA</a>. Beyond this, the supplement is also compatible with a <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism" title="Vegetarianism">vegetarian</a> diet.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Its DHA content is roughly equivalent to that of <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a> based fish oil.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuels">Fuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Fuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Algae can be converted into various types of fuels, depending on the technique and the part of the cells used. The <a href="/wiki/Lipid" title="Lipid">lipid</a>, or oily part of the algae biomass can be extracted and converted into biodiesel through a process similar to that used for any other vegetable oil, or converted in a refinery into "drop-in" replacements for petroleum-based fuels. Alternatively or following lipid extraction, the <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrate</a> content of algae can be fermented into <a href="/wiki/Bioethanol" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioethanol">bioethanol</a> or <a href="/wiki/Butanol_fuel" title="Butanol fuel">butanol fuel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-merinews_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merinews-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Biodiesel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</a></div>
<p>Biodiesel is a diesel fuel derived from animal or plant lipids (oils and fats). Studies have shown that some species of algae can produce 60% or more of their dry weight in the form of oil.<sup id="cite_ref-doi10.1007.2FBF00410827_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1007.2FBF00410827-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1_30-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sheehan_J._1998._p._1-30">[30]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tornabene.2C_1983_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tornabene.2C_1983-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Because the cells grow in aqueous suspension, where they have more efficient access to water, <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> and dissolved nutrients, microalgae are capable of producing large amounts of biomass and usable oil in either high rate algal ponds or <a href="/wiki/Photobioreactor" title="Photobioreactor">photobioreactors</a>. This oil can then be turned into <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> which could be sold for use in automobiles. Regional production of microalgae and processing into biofuels will provide economic benefits to rural communities.<sup id="cite_ref-SARDI_AQUATIC_SCIENCES_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SARDI_AQUATIC_SCIENCES-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>As they do not have to produce structural compounds such as cellulose for leaves, stems, or roots, and because they can be grown floating in a rich nutritional medium, microalgae can have faster growth rates than terrestrial crops. Also, they can convert a much higher fraction of their biomass to oil than conventional crops, e.g. 60% versus 2-3% for soybeans.<sup id="cite_ref-Tornabene.2C_1983_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tornabene.2C_1983-40">[40]</a></sup> The per unit area yield of oil from algae is estimated to be from 58,700 to 136,900 L/ha/year, depending on lipid content, which is 10 to 23 times as high as the next highest yielding crop, oil palm, at 5,950 L/ha/year.<sup id="cite_ref-doi10.1016.2Fj.rser.2012.01.003_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1016.2Fj.rser.2012.01.003-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Aquatic_Species_Program" title="Aquatic Species Program">The U.S. Department of Energy's Aquatic Species Program</a>, 1978–1996, focused on biodiesel from microalgae. The final report suggested that <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">biodiesel</a> could be the only viable method by which to produce enough fuel to replace current world diesel usage.<sup id="cite_ref-BiodieselFromAlgae_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BiodieselFromAlgae-45">[45]</a></sup> If algae-derived biodiesel were to replace the annual global production of 1.1bn tons of conventional diesel then a land mass of 57.3 million hectares would be required, which would be highly favorable compared to other biofuels.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biobutanol">Biobutanol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Biobutanol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Butanol_fuel" title="Butanol fuel">Butanol fuel</a></div>
<p>Butanol can be made from <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a> or <a href="/wiki/Diatom" title="Diatom">diatoms</a> using only a solar powered <a href="/wiki/Biorefinery" title="Biorefinery">biorefinery</a>. This fuel has an <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> 10% less than gasoline, and greater than that of either <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">ethanol</a> or <a href="/wiki/Methanol_fuel" title="Methanol fuel">methanol</a>. In most gasoline engines, butanol can be used in place of gasoline with no modifications. In several tests, butanol consumption is similar to that of gasoline, and when blended with gasoline, provides better performance and corrosion resistance than that of ethanol or <a href="/wiki/E85" title="E85">E85</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>The green waste left over from the algae oil extraction can be used to produce butanol. In addition, it has been shown that macroalgae (seaweeds) can be fermented by <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridia" title="Clostridia">Clostridia</a></i> genus bacteria to butanol and other solvents.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biogasoline">Biogasoline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Biogasoline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Biogasoline" title="Biogasoline">Biogasoline</a> is gasoline produced from <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>. Like traditionally produced gasoline, it contains between 6 (<a href="/wiki/Hexane" title="Hexane">hexane</a>) and 12 (<a href="/wiki/Dodecane" title="Dodecane">dodecane</a>) carbon atoms per molecule and can be used in <a href="/wiki/Internal-combustion_engine" class="mw-redirect" title="Internal-combustion engine">internal-combustion engines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methane">Methane</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Methane">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-MethanProductionFromAlgae_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MethanProductionFromAlgae-50">[50]</a></sup> the main constituent of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> can be produced from algae in various methods, namely <a href="/wiki/Gasification" title="Gasification">Gasification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">Pyrolysis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_Digestion" class="mw-redirect" title="Anaerobic Digestion">Anaerobic Digestion</a>. In Gasification and Pyrolysis methods methane is extracted under high temperature and pressure. Anaerobic Digestion<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> is a straightforward method involved in decomposition of algae into simple components then transforming it into <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acids" class="mw-redirect" title="Fatty acids">fatty acids</a> using <a href="/wiki/Microbes" class="mw-redirect" title="Microbes">microbes</a> like acidific bacteria followed by removing any solid particles and finally adding <a href="/wiki/Methanogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Methanogenic">methanogenic</a> bacteria to release a gas mixture containing methane. A number of studies have successfully shown that biomass from microalgae can be converted into biogas via anaerobic digestion.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Therefore, in order to improve the overall energy balance of microalgae cultivation operations, it has been proposed to recover the energy contained in waste biomass via anaerobic digestion to methane for generating electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethanol">Ethanol</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ethanol">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Algenol" title="Algenol">Algenol</a> system which is being commercialized by <a href="/wiki/BioFields_(Renewable_and_Sustainable_Energy)" class="mw-redirect" title="BioFields (Renewable and Sustainable Energy)">BioFields</a> in <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Libertad_(Sonora)" class="mw-redirect" title="Puerto Libertad (Sonora)">Puerto Libertad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sonora" title="Sonora">Sonora</a>, Mexico utilizes seawater and industrial exhaust to produce ethanol. <a href="/wiki/Porphyridium_cruentum" title="Porphyridium cruentum">Porphyridium cruentum</a> also have shown to be potentially suitable for ethanol production due to its capacity for accumulating large amount of carbohydrates.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Green_Diesel">Green Diesel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Green Diesel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_refining" title="Vegetable oil refining">Vegetable oil refining</a></div>
<p>Algae can be used to produce '<a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil_refining" title="Vegetable oil refining">green diesel</a>' (also known as renewable diesel, hydrotreating vegetable oil<sup id="cite_ref-alpha_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alpha-59">[59]</a></sup> or hydrogen-derived renewable diesel)<sup id="cite_ref-seven_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seven-60">[60]</a></sup> through a hydrotreating refinery process that breaks molecules down into shorter <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> chains used in <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> engines.<sup id="cite_ref-alpha_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alpha-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> It has the same chemical properties as petroleum-based diesel<sup id="cite_ref-alpha_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alpha-59">[59]</a></sup> meaning that it does not require new engines, pipelines or infrastructure to distribute and use. It has yet to be produced at a cost that is competitive with <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-seven_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seven-60">[60]</a></sup> While hydrotreating is currently the most common pathway to produce fuel-like hydrocarbons via decarboxylation/decarbonylation, there is an alternative process offering a number of important advantages over hydrotreating. In this regard, the work of Crocker et al.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-62">[62]</a></sup> and Lercher et al.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> is particularly noteworthy. for of oil refining, research is underway for catalytic conversion of <a href="/wiki/Renewable_fuels_by_decarboxylation" class="mw-redirect" title="Renewable fuels by decarboxylation">renewable fuels by decarboxylation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-64">[64]</a></sup> As the oxygen is present in crude oil at rather low levels, of the order of 0.5%, deoxygenation in petroleum refining is not of</p>
<p>much concern, and no catalysts are specifically formulated for oxygenates hydrotreating. Hence, one of the critical technical challenges to make the hydrodeoxygenation of algae oil process economically feasible is related to the research and development of effective catalysts.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jet_fuel">Jet fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Jet fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Aviation_biofuel" title="Aviation biofuel">Aviation biofuel</a></div>
<p>Rising <a href="/wiki/Jet_fuel" title="Jet fuel">jet fuel</a> prices are putting severe pressure on airline companies,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> creating an incentive for algal jet fuel research. The International Air Transport Association, for example, supports research, development and deployment of algal fuels. IATA's goal is for its members to be using 10% alternative fuels by 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Trials have been carried with aviation biofuel by <a href="/wiki/Air_New_Zealand" title="Air New Zealand">Air New Zealand</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lufthansa" title="Lufthansa">Lufthansa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Virgin_Atlantic_Airways" class="mw-redirect" title="Virgin Atlantic Airways">Virgin Airlines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>In February 2010, the <a href="/wiki/Defense_Advanced_Research_Projects_Agency" class="mw-redirect" title="Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency">Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency</a> announced that the U.S. military was about to begin large-scale oil production from algal ponds into jet fuel. After extraction at a cost of $2 per gallon, the oil will be refined at less than $3 a gallon. A larger-scale refining operation, producing 50 million gallons a year, is expected to go into production in 2013, with the possibility of lower per gallon costs so that algae-based fuel would be competitive with fossil fuels. The projects, run by the companies <a href="/wiki/Science_Applications_International_Corporation" title="Science Applications International Corporation">SAIC</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_Atomics" title="General Atomics">General Atomics</a>, are expected to produce 1,000 gallons of oil per acre per year from algal ponds.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Species">Species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Research into algae for the mass-production of oil focuses mainly on <a href="/wiki/Microalgae" class="mw-redirect" title="Microalgae">microalgae</a> (organisms capable of photosynthesis that are less than 0.4&#160;mm in diameter, including the <a href="/wiki/Diatoms" class="mw-redirect" title="Diatoms">diatoms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">cyanobacteria</a>) as opposed to macroalgae, such as <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a>. The preference for microalgae has come about due largely to their less complex structure, fast growth rates, and high oil-content (for some species). However, some research is being done into using seaweeds for biofuels, probably due to the high availability of this resource.<sup id="cite_ref-Times_Online_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Times_Online-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> researchers across various locations worldwide have started investigating the following species for their suitability as a mass oil-producers:<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Botryococcus_braunii" title="Botryococcus braunii">Botryococcus braunii</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Chlorella" title="Chlorella">Chlorella</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Dunaliella_tertiolecta" class="mw-redirect" title="Dunaliella tertiolecta">Dunaliella tertiolecta</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Gracilaria" title="Gracilaria">Gracilaria</a></i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Pleurochrysis_carterae" title="Pleurochrysis carterae">Pleurochrysis carterae</a></i> (also called CCMP647).<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Sargassum" title="Sargassum">Sargassum</a></i>, with 10 times the output volume of <i>Gracilaria</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The amount of oil each strain of algae produces varies widely. Note the following microalgae and their various oil yields:</p>
<ul>
<li><i>Ankistrodesmus</i> TR-87: 28–40% dry weight</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Botryococcus_braunii" title="Botryococcus braunii">Botryococcus braunii</a></i>: 29–75% dw</li>
<li><i>Chlorella</i> sp.: 29%dw</li>
<li><i>Chlorella protothecoides</i>(autotrophic/ heterothrophic): 15–55% dw</li>
<li><i>Crypthecodinium cohnii</i>: 20%dw</li>
<li><i>Cyclotella</i> DI- 35: 42%dw</li>
<li><i>Dunaliella tertiolecta</i>&#160;: 36–42%dw</li>
<li><i>Hantzschia</i> DI-160: 66%dw</li>
<li><i>Nannochloris</i>: 31(6–63)%dw</li>
<li><i>Nannochloropsis</i>&#160;: 46(31–68)%dw</li>
<li><i>Neochloris oleoabundans</i>: 35–54%dw</li>
<li><i>Nitzschia</i> TR-114: 28–50%dw</li>
<li><i>Phaeodactylum tricornutum</i>: 31%dw</li>
<li><i>Scenedesmus</i> TR-84: 45%dw</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Schizochytrium" title="Schizochytrium">Schizochytrium</a></i> 50–77%dw<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Stichococcus</i>: 33(9–59)%dw</li>
<li><i>Tetraselmis suecica</i>: 15–32%dw</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Thalassiosira_pseudonana" title="Thalassiosira pseudonana">Thalassiosira pseudonana</a></i>: (21–31)%dw</li>
</ul>
<p>In addition, due to its high growth-rate, <i><a href="/wiki/Sea_lettuce" title="Sea lettuce">Ulva</a></i><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> has been investigated as a fuel for use in the <i>SOFT cycle</i>, (SOFT stands for Solar Oxygen Fuel Turbine), a closed-cycle power-generation system suitable for use in arid, subtropical regions.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Algae_Nutrients_and_Growth_Inputs">Algae Nutrients and Growth Inputs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Algae Nutrients and Growth Inputs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Light is what algae primarily needs for growth as it is the most limiting factor. Many companies are investing for developing systems and technologies for providing artificial light. One of them is OriginOil that has developed a Helix BioReactorTM that features a rotating vertical shaft with low-energy lights arranged in a helix pattern.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-82">[82]</a></sup> Water temperature also influences the metabolic and reproductive rates of algae. Although most algae species grow at low rate when the water temperature gets lower, the biomass of algal communities can get large due to the absence of grazing organisms.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-82">[82]</a></sup> The modest increases in water current velocity may also affect rates of algae growth since the rate of nutrient uptake and boundary layer diffusion increases with current velocity.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other than light and water, phosphorus, nitrogen, and certain micronutrients are also useful and essential in growing algae. Nitrogen and phosphorus are the two most significant nutrients required for algal productivity, but other nutrients such as carbon and silica are additionally required.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Of the nutrients required, phosphorus is one of the most essential ones as it is used in numerous metabolic processes. The microalgae <i>D. tertiolecta</i> was analyzed to see which nutrient affects its growth the most.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-84">[84]</a></sup> The concentrations of phosphorus (P), iron (Fe), cobalt (Co), zinc (Zn), manganese (Mn) and molybdenum (Mo), magnesium (Mg), calcium (Ca), silicon (Si) and sulfur (S) concentrations were measured daily using inductively coupled plasma (ICP) analysis. Among all these elements being measured, phosphorus resulted in the most dramatic decrease, with a reduction of 84% over the course of the culture.<sup id="cite_ref-:3_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-84">[84]</a></sup> This result indicates that phosphorus, in the form of phosphate, is required in high amounts by all organisms for metabolism.</p>
<p>There are two enrichment media that have been extensively used to grow most of the algae species: Walne medium and the Guillard’s F/<sub>2</sub> medium.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-85">[85]</a></sup> These commercially available nutrient solutions may reduce time for preparing all the nutrients required to grow algae. However, due to their complexity in the process of generation and high cost, they are not used for large-scale culture operations.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-85">[85]</a></sup> Therefore, enrichment media used for mass production of algae contain only the most important nutrients with agriculture-grade fertilizers rather than laboratory-grade fertilizers.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Algae_cultivation">Algae cultivation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Algae cultivation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_microalgae_in_hatcheries" title="Culture of microalgae in hatcheries">Culture of microalgae in hatcheries</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Photobioreactor_PBR_4000_G_IGV_Biotech.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Photobioreactor_PBR_4000_G_IGV_Biotech.jpg/220px-Photobioreactor_PBR_4000_G_IGV_Biotech.jpg" width="220" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Photobioreactor_PBR_4000_G_IGV_Biotech.jpg/330px-Photobioreactor_PBR_4000_G_IGV_Biotech.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Photobioreactor_PBR_4000_G_IGV_Biotech.jpg/440px-Photobioreactor_PBR_4000_G_IGV_Biotech.jpg 2x" data-file-width="886" data-file-height="623" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Photobioreactor_PBR_4000_G_IGV_Biotech.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Photobioreactor from glass tubes</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Algal_open_pond_design.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Algal_open_pond_design.jpg/220px-Algal_open_pond_design.jpg" width="220" height="125" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Algal_open_pond_design.jpg/330px-Algal_open_pond_design.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Algal_open_pond_design.jpg/440px-Algal_open_pond_design.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1006" data-file-height="573" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Algal_open_pond_design.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Design of a race-way open pond commonly used for algal culture</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Algae grow much faster than food crops, and can produce hundreds of times more oil per unit area than conventional crops such as rapeseed, palms, soybeans, or <a href="/wiki/Jatropha" title="Jatropha">jatropha</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-doi10.1016.2Fj.rser.2012.01.003_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1016.2Fj.rser.2012.01.003-44">[44]</a></sup> As algae have a harvesting cycle of 1–10 days, their cultivation permits several harvests in a very short time-frame, a strategy differing from that associated with annual crops.<sup id="cite_ref-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294-41">[41]</a></sup> In addition, algae can be grown on land unsuitable for terrestrial crops, including arid land and land with excessively saline soil, minimizing competition with agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-doi10.1007.2Fs12155-008-9008-8_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1007.2Fs12155-008-9008-8-86">[86]</a></sup> Most research on algae cultivation has focused on growing algae in clean but expensive <a href="/wiki/Photobioreactor" title="Photobioreactor">photobioreactors</a>, or in open ponds, which are cheap to maintain but prone to contamination.<sup id="cite_ref-Mata.2C_T.M._2009_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mata.2C_T.M._2009-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Closed-loop_system">Closed-loop system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Closed-loop system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The lack of equipment and structures needed to begin growing algae in large quantities has inhibited widespread mass-production of algae for biofuel production. Maximum use of existing agriculture processes and hardware is the goal.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>Closed systems (not exposed to open air) avoid the problem of contamination by other organisms blown in by the air. The problem for a closed system is finding a cheap source of sterile <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>. Several experimenters have found the <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> from a smokestack works well for growing algae.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> For reasons of economy, some experts think that algae farming for biofuels will have to be done as part of <a href="/wiki/Cogeneration" title="Cogeneration">cogeneration</a>, where it can make use of waste heat and help soak up pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-Worldwatch_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worldwatch-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pond_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pond-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Photobioreactors">Photobioreactors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Photobioreactors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Most companies pursuing algae as a source of biofuels pump <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">nutrient</a>-rich water through plastic or borosilicate glass tubes (called "<a href="/wiki/Bioreactor" title="Bioreactor">bioreactors</a>" ) that are exposed to sunlight (and so-called <a href="/wiki/Photobioreactor" title="Photobioreactor">photobioreactors</a> or PBR).</p>
<p>Running a PBR is more difficult than using an open pond, and costlier, but may provide a higher level of control and productivity.<sup id="cite_ref-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294-41">[41]</a></sup> In addition, a photobioreactor can be integrated into a closed loop cogeneration system much more easily than ponds or other methods.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Open_pond">Open pond</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Open pond">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Microalgenkwekerij_te_Heure_bij_Borculo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Microalgenkwekerij_te_Heure_bij_Borculo.jpg/220px-Microalgenkwekerij_te_Heure_bij_Borculo.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Microalgenkwekerij_te_Heure_bij_Borculo.jpg/330px-Microalgenkwekerij_te_Heure_bij_Borculo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Microalgenkwekerij_te_Heure_bij_Borculo.jpg/440px-Microalgenkwekerij_te_Heure_bij_Borculo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Microalgenkwekerij_te_Heure_bij_Borculo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Raceway pond used for the cultivation of microalgae</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Open-pond systems for the most part have been given up for the cultivation of algae with especially high oil content.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Many<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (September 2012)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> believe that a major flaw of the <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_Species_Program" title="Aquatic Species Program">Aquatic Species Program</a> was the decision to focus their efforts exclusively on open-ponds; this makes the entire effort dependent upon the hardiness of the strain chosen, requiring it to be unnecessarily resilient in order to withstand wide swings in temperature and pH, and competition from invasive algae and bacteria. Open systems using a monoculture are also vulnerable to viral infection. The energy that a high-oil strain invests into the production of oil is energy that is not invested into the production of proteins or carbohydrates, usually resulting in the species being less hardy, or having a slower growth rate. Algal species with a lower oil content, not having to divert their energies away from growth, can be grown more effectively in the harsher conditions of an open system.<sup id="cite_ref-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some open sewage-ponds trial production has taken place in <a href="/wiki/Marlborough_Region" title="Marlborough Region">Marlborough</a>, New Zealand.<sup id="cite_ref-BiodieselAlgaeSeweragePonds_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BiodieselAlgaeSeweragePonds-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algal_turf_scrubber">Algal turf scrubber</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Algal turf scrubber">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ATSHydromentia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/ATSHydromentia.jpg/220px-ATSHydromentia.jpg" width="220" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/64/ATSHydromentia.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="223" data-file-height="204" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ATSHydromentia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
2.5 acre ATS system, installed by Hydromentia on a farm creek in Florida</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The algal turf scrubber (ATS) is a system designed primarily for cleaning nutrients and pollutants out of water using algal turfs. ATS mimics the algal turfs of a natural coral reef by taking in nutrient rich water from waste streams or natural water sources, and pulsing it over a sloped surface.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> This surface is coated with a rough plastic membrane or a screen, which allows naturally occurring algal spores to settle and colonize the surface. Once the algae has been established, it can be harvested every 5–15 days,<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> and can produce 18 metric tons of algal biomass per hectare per year.<sup id="cite_ref-bioscience.oxfordjournals.org_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bioscience.oxfordjournals.org-97">[97]</a></sup> In contrast to other methods, which focus primarily on a single high yielding species of algae, this method focuses on naturally occurring polycultures of algae. As such, the lipid content of the algae in an ATS system is usually lower, which makes it more suitable for a fermented fuel product, such as ethanol, methane, or butanol.<sup id="cite_ref-bioscience.oxfordjournals.org_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bioscience.oxfordjournals.org-97">[97]</a></sup> Conversely, the harvested algae could be treated with a <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_liquefaction" title="Hydrothermal liquefaction">hydrothermal liquefaction</a> process, which would make possible biodiesel, gasoline, and jet fuel production.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are three major advantages of ATS over other systems. The first advantage is documented higher productivity over open pond systems.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The second is lower operating and fuel production costs. The third is the elimination of contamination issues due to the reliance on naturally occurring algae species. The projected costs for energy production in an ATS system are $0.75/kg, compared to a photobioreactor which would cost $3.50/kg.<sup id="cite_ref-bioscience.oxfordjournals.org_97-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bioscience.oxfordjournals.org-97">[97]</a></sup> Furthermore, due to the fact that the primary purpose of ATS is removing nutrients and pollutants out of water, and these costs have been shown to be lower than other methods of nutrient removal, this may incentivize the use of this technology for nutrient removal as the primary function, with biofuel production as an added benefit.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Algae_ATS_harvest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Algae_ATS_harvest.jpg/220px-Algae_ATS_harvest.jpg" width="220" height="160" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Algae_ATS_harvest.jpg/330px-Algae_ATS_harvest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/27/Algae_ATS_harvest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="408" data-file-height="296" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Algae_ATS_harvest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Algae being harvested and dried from an ATS system</div>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fuel_production">Fuel production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Fuel production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>After harvesting the algae, the biomass is typically processed in a series of steps, which can differ based on the species and desired product; this is an active area of research<sup id="cite_ref-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294_41-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294-41">[41]</a></sup> and also is the bottleneck of this technology: the cost of extraction is higher than those obtained. One of the solutions is to use filter feeders to "eat" them. Improved animals can provide both foods and fuels. An alternative method to extract the algae is to grow the algae with specific types of fungi. This causes bio-flocculation of the algae which allows for easier extraction.<sup id="cite_ref-MackayGomes2015_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MackayGomes2015-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dehydration">Dehydration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Dehydration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Often, the algae is dehydrated, and then a solvent such as hexane is used to extract energy-rich compounds like <a href="/wiki/Triglyceride" title="Triglyceride">triglycerides</a> from the dried material.<sup id="cite_ref-doi10.1016.2Fj.copbio.2010.03.005_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1016.2Fj.copbio.2010.03.005-1">[1]</a></sup> Then, the extracted compounds can be processed into fuel using standard industrial procedures. For example, the extracted triglycerides are reacted with methanol to create biodiesel via <a href="/wiki/Transesterification" title="Transesterification">transesterification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-doi10.1016.2Fj.copbio.2010.03.005_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1016.2Fj.copbio.2010.03.005-1">[1]</a></sup> The unique composition of fatty acids of each species influences the quality of the resulting biodiesel and thus must be taken into account when selecting algal species for feedstock.<sup id="cite_ref-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294_41-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hydrothermal_liquefaction">Hydrothermal liquefaction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Hydrothermal liquefaction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An alternative approach called <a href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_liquefaction" title="Hydrothermal liquefaction">Hydrothermal liquefaction</a> employs a continuous process that subjects harvested wet algae to high temperatures and pressures—350&#160;°C (662&#160;°F) and 3,000 pounds per square inch (21,000&#160;kPa).<sup id="cite_ref-giz1312_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giz1312-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Products include crude oil, which can be further refined into aviation fuel, gasoline, or diesel fuel using one or many upgrading processes.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The test process converted between 50 and 70 percent of the algae’s carbon into fuel. Other outputs include clean water, fuel gas and nutrients such as nitrogen, phosphorus, and potassium.<sup id="cite_ref-giz1312_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giz1312-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrients">Nutrients</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Nutrients">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Algal_nutrient_solutions" class="mw-redirect" title="Algal nutrient solutions">Algal nutrient solutions</a></div>
<p>Nutrients like <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> (N), <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a> (P), and <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a> (K), are important for plant growth and are essential parts of fertilizer. <a href="/wiki/Silica" class="mw-redirect" title="Silica">Silica</a> and iron, as well as several trace elements, may also be considered important marine nutrients as the lack of one can limit the growth of, or productivity in, an area.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carbon_dioxide">Carbon dioxide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Carbon dioxide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Bubbling <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> through algal cultivation systems can greatly increase productivity and yield (up to a saturation point). Typically, about 1.8 tonnes of <a href="/wiki/CO2" class="mw-redirect" title="CO2"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></a> will be utilised per tonne of algal biomass (dry) produced, though this varies with algae species.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The Glenturret Distillery in <a href="/wiki/Perthshire" title="Perthshire">Perthshire</a>, UK – home to <a href="/wiki/The_Famous_Grouse" title="The Famous Grouse">The Famous Grouse</a> Whisky – percolate <a href="/wiki/CO2" class="mw-redirect" title="CO2"><span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span></a> made during the whisky distillation through a microalgae bioreactor. Each tonne of microalgae absorbs two tonnes of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span>. Scottish Bioenergy, who run the project, sell the microalgae as high value, protein-rich food for <a href="/wiki/Fishery" title="Fishery">fisheries</a>. In the future, they will use the algae residues to produce renewable energy through <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digestion" title="Anaerobic digestion">anaerobic digestion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ecologist_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ecologist-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nitrogen">Nitrogen</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Nitrogen">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nitrogen is a valuable substrate that can be utilized in algal growth. Various sources of nitrogen can be used as a nutrient for algae, with varying capacities. Nitrate was found to be the preferred source of nitrogen, in regards to amount of biomass grown. Urea is a readily available source that shows comparable results, making it an economical substitute for nitrogen source in large scale culturing of algae.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Despite the clear increase in growth in comparison to a nitrogen-less medium, it has been shown that alterations in nitrogen levels affect lipid content within the algal cells. In one study<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> nitrogen deprivation for 72 hours caused the total fatty acid content (on a per cell basis) to increase by 2.4-fold. 65% of the total fatty acids were esterified to triacylglycerides in oil bodies, when compared to the initial culture, indicating that the algal cells utilized de novo synthesis of fatty acids. It is vital for the lipid content in algal cells to be of high enough quantity, while maintaining adequate cell division times, so parameters that can maximize both are under investigation.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wastewater">Wastewater</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Wastewater">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wastewater_treatment_facility" class="mw-redirect" title="Wastewater treatment facility">Wastewater treatment facility</a></div>
<p>A possible nutrient source is <a href="/wiki/Waste_water" class="mw-redirect" title="Waste water">waste water</a> from the treatment of sewage, agricultural, or flood plain run-off, all currently major pollutants and health risks. However, this waste water cannot feed algae directly and must first be processed by bacteria, through <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_digestion" title="Anaerobic digestion">anaerobic digestion</a>. If waste water is not processed before it reaches the algae, it will contaminate the algae in the reactor, and at the very least, kill much of the desired algae strain. In <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">biogas</a> facilities, organic waste is often converted to a mixture of carbon dioxide, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, and organic fertilizer. Organic fertilizer that comes out of the digester is liquid, and nearly suitable for algae growth, but it must first be cleaned and sterilized.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>The utilization of wastewater and ocean water instead of freshwater is strongly advocated due to the continuing depletion of freshwater resources. However, heavy metals, trace metals, and other contaminants in wastewater can decrease the ability of cells to produce lipids biosynthetically and also impact various other workings in the machinery of cells. The same is true for ocean water, but the contaminants are found in different concentrations. Thus, agricultural-grade fertilizer is the preferred source of nutrients, but heavy metals are again a problem, especially for strains of algae that are susceptible to these metals. In open pond systems the use of strains of algae that can deal with high concentrations of heavy metals could prevent other organisms from infesting these systems.<sup id="cite_ref-doi10.1007.2Fs12155-008-9008-8_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1007.2Fs12155-008-9008-8-86">[86]</a></sup> In some instances it has even been shown that strains of algae can remove over 90% of nickel and zinc from industrial wastewater in relatively short periods of time.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Environmental impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In comparison with terrestrial-based biofuel crops such as corn or soybeans, microalgal production results in a much less significant land footprint due to the higher oil productivity from the microalgae than all other oil crops.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-113">[113]</a></sup> Algae can also be grown on marginal lands useless for ordinary crops and with low conservation value, and can use water from salt aquifers that is not useful for agriculture or drinking.<sup id="cite_ref-Worldwatch_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worldwatch-91">[91]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-114">[114]</a></sup> Algae can also grow on the surface of the ocean in bags or floating screens.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> Thus microalgae could provide a source of clean energy with little impact on the provisioning of adequate food and water or the conservation of biodiversity.<sup id="cite_ref-doi10.1111.2Fj.1523-1739.2007.00879.x_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1111.2Fj.1523-1739.2007.00879.x-116">[116]</a></sup> Algae cultivation also requires no external subsidies of insecticides or herbicides, removing any risk of generating associated pesticide waste streams. In addition, algal biofuels are much less toxic, and degrade far more readily than petroleum-based fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> However, due to the flammable nature of any combustible fuel, there is potential for some environmental hazards if ignited or spilled, as may occur in a train derailment or a pipeline leak.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> This hazard is reduced compared to fossil fuels, due to the ability for algal biofuels to be produced in a much more localized manner, and due to the lower toxicity overall, but the hazard is still there nonetheless. Therefore, algal biofuels should be treated in a similar manner to petroleum fuels in transportation and use, with sufficient safety measures in place at all times.</p>
<p>Studies have determined that replacing fossil fuels with renewable energy sources, such as biofuels, have the capability of reducing <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> emissions by up to 80%.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> An algae-based system could capture approximately 80% of the <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> emitted from a power plant when sunlight is available. Although this <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> will later be released into the atmosphere when the fuel is burned, this <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> would have entered the atmosphere regardless.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-114">[114]</a></sup> The possibility of reducing total <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> emissions therefore lies in the prevention of the release of <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> from fossil fuels. Furthermore, compared to fuels like diesel and petroleum, and even compared to other sources of biofuels, the production and combustion of algal biofuel does not produce any sulfur oxides or nitrous oxides, and produces a reduced amount of carbon monoxide, unburned hydrocarbons, and reduced emission of other harmful pollutants.<sup id="cite_ref-Hemaiswarya.2C_S._1135_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hemaiswarya.2C_S._1135-122">[122]</a></sup> Since terrestrial plant sources of biofuel production simply do not have the production capacity to meet current energy requirements, microalgae may be one of the only options to approach complete replacement of fossil fuels.</p>
<p>Microalgae production also includes the ability to use saline waste or waste <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> streams as an energy source. This opens a new strategy to produce biofuel in conjunction with waste water treatment, while being able to produce clean water as a byproduct.<sup id="cite_ref-Hemaiswarya.2C_S._1135_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hemaiswarya.2C_S._1135-122">[122]</a></sup> When used in a microalgal bioreactor, harvested microalgae will capture significant quantities of organic compounds as well as heavy metal contaminants absorbed from wastewater streams that would otherwise be directly discharged into surface and ground-water.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_113-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-113">[113]</a></sup> Moreover, this process also allows the recovery of phosphorus from waste, which is an essential but scarce element in nature – the reserves of which are estimated to have depleted in the last 50 years.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Another possibility is the use of algae production systems to clean up non-point source pollution, in a system known as an algal turf scrubber (ATS). This has been demonstrated to reduce nitrogen and phosphorus levels in rivers and other large bodies of water affected by eutrophication, and systems are being built that will be capable of processing up to 110 million liters of water per day. ATS can also be used for treating point source pollution, such as the waste water mentioned above, or in treating livestock effluent.<sup id="cite_ref-bioscience.oxfordjournals.org_97-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bioscience.oxfordjournals.org-97">[97]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polycultures">Polycultures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Polycultures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nearly all research in algal biofuels has focused on culturing single species, or monocultures, of microalgae. However, ecological theory and empirical studies have demonstrated that plant and algae polycultures, i.e. groups of multiple species, tend to produce larger yields than monocultures.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> Experiments have also shown that more diverse aquatic microbial communities tend to be more stable through time than less diverse communities.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> Recent studies found that polycultures of microalgae produced significantly higher lipid yields than monocultures.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Polycultures also tend to be more resistant to pest and disease outbreaks, as well as invasion by other plants or algae.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Thus culturing microalgae in polyculture may not only increase yields and stability of yields of biofuel, but also reduce the environmental impact of an algal biofuel industry.<sup id="cite_ref-doi10.1111.2Fj.1523-1739.2007.00879.x_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1111.2Fj.1523-1739.2007.00879.x-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_viability">Economic viability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Economic viability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There is clearly a demand for sustainable biofuel production, but whether a particular biofuel will be used ultimately depends not on sustainability but cost efficiency. Therefore, research is focusing on cutting the cost of algal biofuel production to the point where it can compete with conventional petroleum.<sup id="cite_ref-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294_41-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tipsstephens_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tipsstephens-137">[137]</a></sup> The production of several products from algae has been mentioned<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="Who says this? (August 2015)">weasel&#160;words</span></a></i>]</sup> as the most important factor for making algae production economically viable. Other factors are the improving of the solar energy to biomass conversion efficiency (currently 3%, but 5 to 7% is theoretically attainable<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>)and making the oil extraction from the algae easier.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 2007 report<sup id="cite_ref-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294_41-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294-41">[41]</a></sup> a formula was derived estimating the cost of algal oil in order for it to be a viable substitute to petroleum diesel:</p>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>C<sub>(algal oil)</sub> = 25.9 × 10<sup>−3</sup> C<sub>(petroleum)</sub></dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>where: C<sub>(algal oil)</sub> is the price of microalgal oil in dollars per gallon and C<sub>(petroleum)</sub> is the price of crude oil in dollars per barrel. This equation assumes that algal oil has roughly 80% of the caloric energy value of crude petroleum.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>With current technology available, it is estimated that the cost of producing microalgal biomass is $2.95/kg for photobioreactors and $3.80/kg for open-ponds. These estimates assume that carbon dioxide is available at no cost.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> If the annual biomass production capacity is increased to 10,000 tonnes, the cost of production per kilogram reduces to roughly $0.47 and $0.60, respectively. Assuming that the biomass contains 30% oil by weight, the cost of biomass for providing a liter of oil would be approximately $1.40 ($5.30/gal) and $1.81 ($6.85/gal) for photobioreactors and raceways, respectively.
