Oil recovered from the lower cost biomass produced in photobioreactors is estimated to cost $2.80/L, assuming the recovery process contributes 50% to the cost of the final recovered oil.<sup id="cite_ref-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294_41-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chisti.2C_Y._2007.2C_pp._294-41">[41]</a></sup> If existing algae projects can achieve biodiesel production price targets of less than $1 per gallon, the United States may realize its goal of replacing up to 20% of transport fuels by 2020 by using environmentally and economically sustainable fuels from algae production.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>Whereas technical problems, such as harvesting, are being addressed successfully by the industry, the high up-front investment of algae-to-biofuels facilities is seen by many as a major obstacle to the success of this technology. Only few studies on the economic viability are publicly available, and must often rely on the little data (often only engineering estimates) available in the public domain. Dmitrov<sup id="cite_ref-Dmitrov_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dmitrov-143">[143]</a></sup> examined the <a href="/wiki/GreenFuel_Technologies_Corporation" title="GreenFuel Technologies Corporation">GreenFuel's</a> <a href="/wiki/Photobioreactor" title="Photobioreactor">photobioreactor</a> and estimated that algae oil would only be competitive at an oil price of $800 per barrel. A study by Alabi et al.<sup id="cite_ref-Alabi_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alabi-144">[144]</a></sup> examined raceways, photobioreactors and anaerobic fermenters to make biofuels from algae and found that photobioreactors are too expensive to make biofuels. Raceways might be cost-effective in warm climates with very low labor costs, and fermenters may become cost-effective subsequent to significant process improvements. The group found that capital cost, labor cost and operational costs (fertilizer, electricity, etc.) by themselves are too high for algae biofuels to be cost-competitive with conventional fuels. Similar results were found by others,<sup id="cite_ref-Steiner_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Steiner-145">[145]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Radmer_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radmer-146">[146]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Carbon_Trust_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carbon_Trust-147">[147]</a></sup> suggesting that unless new, cheaper ways of harnessing algae for biofuels production are found, their great technical potential may never become economically accessible. Recently, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Rodrigo_E._Teixeira&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Rodrigo E. Teixeira (page does not exist)">Rodrigo E. Teixeira</a><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> demonstrated a new reaction and proposed a process for harvesting and extracting raw materials for biofuel and chemical production that requires a fraction of the energy of current methods, while extracting all cell constituents.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_Byproducts">Use of Byproducts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Use of Byproducts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many of the byproducts produced in the processing of microalgae can be used in various applications, many of which have a longer history of production than algal biofuel. Some of the products not used in the production of biofuel include natural dyes and pigments, antioxidants, and other high-value bio-active compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Mata.2C_T.M._2009_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mata.2C_T.M._2009-87">[87]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> These chemicals and excess biomass have found numerous use in other industries. For example, the dyes and oils have found a place in cosmetics, commonly as thickening and water-binding agents.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> Discoveries within the pharmaceutical industry include antibiotics and antifungals derived from microalgae, as well as natural health products, which have been growing in popularity over the past few decades. For instance <a href="/wiki/Spirulina_(genus)" title="Spirulina (genus)"><i>Spirulina</i></a> contains numerous polyunsaturated fats (Omega 3 and 6), amino acids, and vitamins,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> as well as pigments that may be beneficial, such as beta-carotene and chlorophyll.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Advantages">Advantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Advantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ease_of_growth">Ease of growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Ease of growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One of the main advantages that using microalgae as the feedstock when compared to more traditional crops is that it can be grown much more easily.<sup id="cite_ref-Demirbas.2C_A._2011_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demirbas.2C_A._2011-154">[154]</a></sup> Algae can be grown in land that would not be considered suitable for the growth of the regularly used crops.<sup id="cite_ref-Mata.2C_T.M._2009_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mata.2C_T.M._2009-87">[87]</a></sup> In addition to this, wastewater that would normally hinder plant growth has been shown to be very effective in growing algae.<sup id="cite_ref-Demirbas.2C_A._2011_154-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demirbas.2C_A._2011-154">[154]</a></sup> Because of this, algae can be grown without taking up arable land that would otherwise be used for producing food crops, and the better resources can be reserved for normal crop production. Microalgae also require fewer resources to grow and little attention is needed, allowing the growth and cultivation of algae to be a very passive process.<sup id="cite_ref-Mata.2C_T.M._2009_87-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mata.2C_T.M._2009-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_on_food">Impact on food</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Impact on food">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many traditional feedstocks for biodiesel, such as corn and palm, are also used as feed for livestock on farms, as well as a valuable source of food for humans. Because of this, using them as biofuel reduces the amount of food available for both, resulting in an increased cost for both the food and the fuel produced. Using algae as a source of biodiesel can alleviate this problem in a number of ways. First, algae is not used as a primary food source for humans, meaning that it can be used solely for fuel and there would be little impact in the food industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Vasudevan.2C_P.T._2008_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vasudevan.2C_P.T._2008-155">[155]</a></sup> Second, many of the waste-product extracts produced during the processing of algae for biofuel can be used as a sufficient animal feed. This is an effective way to minimize waste and a much cheaper alternative to the more traditional corn- or grain-based feeds.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minimization_of_waste">Minimization of waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Minimization of waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Growing algae as a source of biofuel has also been shown to have numerous environmental benefits, and has presented itself as a much more environmentally friendly alternative to current biofuels. For one, it is able to utilize run-off, water contaminated with fertilizers and other nutrients that are a by-product of farming, as its primary source of water and nutrients.<sup id="cite_ref-Demirbas.2C_A._2011_154-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demirbas.2C_A._2011-154">[154]</a></sup> Because of this, it prevents this contaminated water from mixing with the lakes and rivers that currently supply our drinking water. In addition to this, the ammonia, nitrates, and phosphates that would normally render the water unsafe actually serve as excellent nutrients for the algae, meaning that fewer resources are needed to grow the algae.<sup id="cite_ref-Mata.2C_T.M._2009_87-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mata.2C_T.M._2009-87">[87]</a></sup> Many algae species used in biodiesel production are excellent bio-fixers, meaning they are able to remove carbon dioxide from the atmosphere to use as a form of energy for themselves. Because of this, they have found use in industry as a way to treat flue gases and reduce GHG emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-Mata.2C_T.M._2009_87-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mata.2C_T.M._2009-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Disadvantages">Disadvantages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Disadvantages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_Viability">Commercial Viability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Commercial Viability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Algae biodiesel is still a fairly new technology. Despite the fact that research began over 30 years ago, it was put on hold during the mid-1990s, mainly due to a lack of funding and a relatively low petroleum cost.<sup id="cite_ref-Pienko.2C_P.T._2009_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pienko.2C_P.T._2009-34">[34]</a></sup> For the next few years algae biofuels saw little attention; it was not until the gas peak of the early 2000s that it eventually had a revitalization in the search for alternative fuel sources.<sup id="cite_ref-Pienko.2C_P.T._2009_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pienko.2C_P.T._2009-34">[34]</a></sup> While the technology exists to harvest and convert algae into a usable source of biodiesel, it still hasn't been implemented into a large enough scale to support the current energy needs. Further research will be required to make the production of algae biofuels more efficient, and at this point it is currently being held back by lobbyists in support of alternative biofuels, like those produced from corn and grain.<sup id="cite_ref-Pienko.2C_P.T._2009_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pienko.2C_P.T._2009-34">[34]</a></sup> In 2013, <a href="/wiki/Exxon_Mobil" class="mw-redirect" title="Exxon Mobil">Exxon Mobil</a> Chairman and CEO <a href="/wiki/Rex_Tillerson" title="Rex Tillerson">Rex Tillerson</a> said that after originally committing to spending up to $600 million on development in a joint venture with <a href="/wiki/J._Craig_Venter" class="mw-redirect" title="J. Craig Venter">J. Craig Venter</a>’s <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_Genomics" title="Synthetic Genomics">Synthetic Genomics</a>, algae is "probably further" than "25 years away" from commercial viability,<sup id="cite_ref-Tillerson_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tillerson-14">[14]</a></sup> although <a href="/wiki/Solazyme" class="mw-redirect" title="Solazyme">Solazyme</a><sup id="cite_ref-Erin_Voegele_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Erin_Voegele-15">[15]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Sapphire_Energy" title="Sapphire Energy">Sapphire Energy</a><sup id="cite_ref-Andrew_Herndon_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andrew_Herndon-16">[16]</a></sup> already began small-scale commercial sales in 2012 and 2013, respectively.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stability">Stability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Stability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The biodiesel produced from the processing of microalgae differs from other forms of biodiesel in the content of polyunsaturated fats.<sup id="cite_ref-Demirbas.2C_A._2011_154-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demirbas.2C_A._2011-154">[154]</a></sup> Polyunsaturated fats are known for their ability to retain fluidity at lower temperatures. While this may seem like an advantage in production during the colder temperatures of the winter, the polyunsaturated fats result in lower stability during regular seasonal temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-Vasudevan.2C_P.T._2008_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vasudevan.2C_P.T._2008-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_projects">Current projects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Current projects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Algae_fuel_in_the_United_States" title="Algae fuel in the United States">Algae fuel in the United States</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> (NREL) is the U.S. Department of Energy's primary national laboratory for renewable energy and energy efficiency research and development. This program is involved in the production of renewable energies and energy efficiency. One of its most current divisions is the biomass program which is involved in biomass characterization, biochemical and thermochemical conversion technologies in conjunction with biomass process engineering and analysis. The program aims at producing energy efficient, cost-effective and environmentally friendly technologies that support rural economies, reduce the nations dependency in oil and improve air quality.<sup id="cite_ref-archive.nrc-cnrc.gc.ca_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archive.nrc-cnrc.gc.ca-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Woods_Hole_Oceanographic_Institution" title="Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution">Woods Hole Oceanographic Institution</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Harbor_Branch_Oceanographic_Institution" class="mw-redirect" title="Harbor Branch Oceanographic Institution">Harbor Branch Oceanographic Institution</a> the wastewater from domestic and industrial sources contain rich organic compounds that are being used to accelerate the growth of algae.<sup id="cite_ref-merinews_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merinews-39">[39]</a></sup> The Department of Biological and Agricultural Engineering at <a href="/wiki/University_of_Georgia" title="University of Georgia">University of Georgia</a> is exploring microalgal biomass production using industrial wastewater.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Algaewheel" class="mw-redirect" title="Algaewheel">Algaewheel</a>, based in <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis" title="Indianapolis">Indianapolis</a>, Indiana, presented a proposal to build a facility in <a href="/wiki/Cedar_Lake,_Indiana" title="Cedar Lake, Indiana">Cedar Lake, Indiana</a> that uses algae to treat <a href="/wiki/Municipal_wastewater" class="mw-redirect" title="Municipal wastewater">municipal wastewater</a>, using the <a href="/wiki/Sludge" title="Sludge">sludge</a> <a href="/wiki/Byproduct" class="mw-redirect" title="Byproduct">byproduct</a> to produce biofuel.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> A similar approach is being followed by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_Systems&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Algae Systems (page does not exist)">Algae Systems</a>, a company based in Daphne, Alabama.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sapphire_Energy" title="Sapphire Energy">Sapphire Energy</a> (San Diego) has produced green crude from algae.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Solazyme" class="mw-redirect" title="Solazyme">Solazyme</a> (<a href="/wiki/South_San_Francisco,_California" title="South San Francisco, California">South San Francisco, California</a>) has produced a fuel suitable for powering jet aircraft from algae.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Universities in the United Kingdom which are working on producing oil from algae include: <a href="/wiki/University_of_Manchester" title="University of Manchester">University of Manchester</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sheffield" title="University of Sheffield">University of Sheffield</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Glasgow" title="University of Glasgow">University of Glasgow</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Brighton" title="University of Brighton">University of Brighton</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_College_London" title="University College London">University College London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imperial_College_London" title="Imperial College London">Imperial College London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cranfield_University" title="Cranfield University">Cranfield University</a> and <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_University" title="Newcastle University">Newcastle University</a>. In Spain, it is also relevant the research carried out by the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_National_Research_Council" title="Spanish National Research Council">CSIC</a>´s <a href="/w/index.php?title=Instituto_de_Bioqu%C3%ADmica_Vegetal_y_Fotos%C3%ADntesis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Instituto de Bioquímica Vegetal y Fotosíntesis (page does not exist)">Instituto de Bioquímica Vegetal y Fotosíntesis</a> (Microalgae <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">Biotechnology</a> Group, <a href="/wiki/Seville" title="Seville">Seville</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Marine Research station in <a href="/wiki/Ketch_Harbour,_Nova_Scotia" title="Ketch Harbour, Nova Scotia">Ketch Harbour, Nova Scotia</a>, has been involved in growing algae for 50 years. The <a href="/wiki/National_Research_Council_(Canada)" title="National Research Council (Canada)">National Research Council (Canada)</a> (NRC) and National Byproducts Program have provided $5 million to fund this project. The aim of the program has been to build a 50 000 litre cultivation pilot plant at the Ketch harbor facility. The station has been involved in assessing how best to grow algae for biofuel and is involved in investigating the utilization of numerous algae species in regions of North America. NRC has joined forces with the United States Department of Energy, the <a href="/wiki/National_Renewable_Energy_Laboratory" title="National Renewable Energy Laboratory">National Renewable Energy Laboratory</a> in Colorado and <a href="/wiki/Sandia_National_Laboratories" title="Sandia National Laboratories">Sandia National Laboratories</a> in New Mexico.<sup id="cite_ref-archive.nrc-cnrc.gc.ca_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-archive.nrc-cnrc.gc.ca-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Algae_Biomass_Association" title="European Algae Biomass Association">European Algae Biomass Association</a> (EABA) is the European association representing both research and industry in the field of algae technologies, currently with 79 members. The association is headquartered in Florence, Italy. The general objective of the EABA is to promote mutual interchange and cooperation in the field of biomass production and use, including biofuels uses and all other utilisations. It aims at creating, developing and maintaining solidarity and links between its Members and at defending their interests at European and international level. Its main target is to act as a catalyst for fostering synergies among scientists, industrialists and decision makers to promote the development of research, technology and industrial capacities in the field of Algae.</p>
<p>CMCL innovations and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a> are carrying out a detailed design study of a C-FAST<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> (Carbon negative Fuels derived from Algal and Solar Technologies) plant. The main objective is to design a pilot plant which can demonstrate production of hydrocarbon fuels (including diesel and gasoline) as sustainable carbon-negative energy carriers and raw materials for the chemical commodity industry. This project will report in June 2013.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> plans to produce biofuel using a special type of algae.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>'s Algae Cluster Project, funded through the <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Framework_Programme" class="mw-redirect" title="Seventh Framework Programme">Seventh Framework Programme</a>, is made up of three algae biofuel projects, each looking to design and build a different algae biofuel facility covering 10ha of land. The projects are BIOFAT, All-Gas and InteSusAl.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since various fuels and chemicals can be produced from algae, it has been suggested to investigate the feasibility of various production processes( conventional extraction/separation, hydrothermal liquefaction, gasification and pyrolysis) for application in an integrated algal biorefinery.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="India">India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Reliance_industries" class="mw-redirect" title="Reliance industries">Reliance industries</a> in collaboration with <a href="/wiki/Algenol" title="Algenol">Algenol</a>, USA commissioned a pilot project to produce algal bio-oil in the year 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Spirulina_(dietary_supplement)" title="Spirulina (dietary supplement)">Spirulina</a> which is an alga rich in proteins content has been commercially cultivated in India. Algae is used in India for treating the sewage in open/natural oxidation ponds This reduces the <a href="/wiki/Biological_Oxygen_Demand" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological Oxygen Demand">Biological Oxygen Demand</a> (BOD) of the sewage and also provides algal biomass which can be converted to fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Algae Biomass Organization (ABO)<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> is a non-profit organization whose mission is "to promote the development of viable commercial markets for renewable and sustainable commodities derived from algae".</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Algae_Association" title="National Algae Association">National Algae Association</a> (NAA) is a non-profit organization of algae researchers, algae production companies and the investment community who share the goal of commercializing algae oil as an alternative feedstock for the biofuels markets. The NAA gives its members a forum to efficiently evaluate various algae technologies for potential early stage company opportunities.</p>
<p>Pond Biofuels Inc.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> in Ontario, Canada has a functioning pilot plant where algae is grown directly off of smokestack emissions from a cement plant, and dried using waste heat.<sup id="cite_ref-Pond_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pond-92">[92]</a></sup> In May 2013, Pond Biofuels announced a partnership with the <a href="/wiki/National_Research_Council_(Canada)" title="National Research Council (Canada)">National Research Council of Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Natural_Resources" title="Canadian Natural Resources">Canadian Natural Resources Limited</a> to construct a demonstration-scale algal biorefinery at an oil sands site near Bonnyville, Alberta.<sup id="cite_ref-nrc-cnrc1_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrc-cnrc1-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ocean_Nutrition_Canada" title="Ocean Nutrition Canada">Ocean Nutrition Canada</a> in Halifax, Nova Scotia, Canada has found a new strain of algae that appears capable of producing oil at a rate 60 times greater than other types of algae being used for the generation of biofuels.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p>
<p>VG Energy, a subsidiary of Viral Genetics Incorporated,<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> claims to have discovered a new method of increasing algal lipid production by disrupting the metabolic pathways that would otherwise divert photosynthetic energy towards carbohydrate production. Using these techniques, the company states that lipid production could be increased several-fold, potentially making algal biofuels cost-competitive with existing fossil fuels.</p>
<p>Algae production from the warm water discharge of a nuclear power plant has been piloted by Patrick C. Kangas at <a href="/wiki/Peach_Bottom_Nuclear_Generating_Station" title="Peach Bottom Nuclear Generating Station">Peach Bottom Nuclear Power Station</a>, owned by <a href="/wiki/Exelon" title="Exelon">Exelon</a> Corporation. This process takes advantage of the relatively high temperature water to sustain algae growth even during winter months.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>Companies such as Sapphire Energy and Bio Solar Cells<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup> are using genetic engineering to make algae fuel production more efficient. According to Klein Lankhorst of Bio Solar Cells, genetic engineering could vastly improve algae fuel efficiency as algae can be modified to only build short carbon chains instead of long chains of carbohydrates.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> Sapphire Energy also uses chemically induced mutations to produce algae suitable for use as a crop.<sup id="cite_ref-domesticated_algae_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-domesticated_algae-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some commercial interests into large-scale algal-cultivation systems are looking to tie in to existing infrastructures, such as cement factories,<sup id="cite_ref-Pond_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pond-92">[92]</a></sup> coal power plants, or sewage treatment facilities. This approach changes wastes into resources to provide the raw materials, <span class="chemf nowrap">CO<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><br />
2</span></span> and nutrients, for the system.<sup id="cite_ref-Sartec_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sartec-179">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>A feasibility study using marine microalgae in a photobioreactor is being done by The International Research Consortium on Continental Margins at the <a href="/wiki/Jacobs_University_Bremen" title="Jacobs University Bremen">Jacobs University Bremen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MarineInternationalUniversityBremen_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarineInternationalUniversityBremen-180">[180]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Department of Environmental Science at <a href="/wiki/Ateneo_de_Manila_University" title="Ateneo de Manila University">Ateneo de Manila University</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, is working on producing biofuel from a local species of algae.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_engineering">Genetic engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Genetic engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">Genetic engineering</a> algae has been used to increase lipid production or growth rates. Current research in genetic engineering includes either the introduction or removal of <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>. In 2007 Oswald et al. introduced a <a href="/w/index.php?title=Monoterpene_synthase&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Monoterpene synthase (page does not exist)">monoterpene synthase</a> from sweet <a href="/wiki/Basil" title="Basil">basil</a> into <a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</a>, a strain of <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> This particular monoterpene synthase causes the de novo synthesis of large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Geraniol" title="Geraniol">geraniol</a>, while also secreting it into the medium. Geraniol is a primary component in <a href="/wiki/Rose_oil" title="Rose oil">rose oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palmarosa_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Palmarosa oil">palmarosa oil</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Citronella_oil" title="Citronella oil">citronella oil</a> as well as essential oils, making it a viable source of <a href="/wiki/Triacylglyceride" class="mw-redirect" title="Triacylglyceride">triacylglycerides</a> for biodiesel production.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>The enzyme <a href="/wiki/ADP-glucose_pyrophosphorylase" class="mw-redirect" title="ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase">ADP-glucose pyrophosphorylase</a> is vital in starch production, but has no connection to lipid synthesis. Removal of this enzyme resulted in the sta6 mutant, which showed increased lipid content. After 18 hours of growth in nitrogen deficient medium the sta6 mutants had on average 17&#160;ng triacylglycerides/1000 cells, compared to 10&#160;ng/1000 cells in WT cells. This increase in lipid production was attributed to reallocation of intracellular resources, as the algae diverted energy from starch production.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013 researchers used a "knock-down" of fat-reducing enzymes (multifunctional lipase/phospholipase/acyltransferase) to increase lipids (oils) without compromising growth. The study also introduced an efficient screening process. Antisense-expressing knockdown strains 1A6 and 1B1 contained 2.4- and 3.3-fold higher lipid content during exponential growth, and 4.1- and 3.2-fold higher lipid content after 40 h of silicon starvation.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Funding_programs">Funding programs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Funding programs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Numerous Funding programs have been created with aims of promoting the use of Renewable Energy. In Canada, the ecoAgriculture biofuels capital initiative (ecoABC) provides $25 million per project to assist farmers in constructing and expanding a renewable fuel production facility. The program has $186 million set aside for these projects. The sustainable development (SDTC) program has also applied $500 millions over 8 years to assist with the construction of next-generation renewable fuels. In addition, over the last 2 years $10 million has been made available for renewable fuel research and analysis<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Europe, the Seventh Framework Programme (FP7) is the main instrument for funding research. Similarly, the NER 300 is an unofficial, independent portal dedicated to renewable energy and grid integration projects. Another program includes the <a href="/wiki/Horizon_2020" title="Horizon 2020">Horizon 2020</a> program which will start 1 January, and will bring together the framework program and other EC innovation and research funding into a new integrated funding system<sup id="cite_ref-biofuelstp.eu_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-biofuelstp.eu-188">[188]</a></sup></p>
<p>The American <a href="/wiki/National_Biodiesel_Board" title="National Biodiesel Board">NBB</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel_in_the_United_States#Feedstock_development" title="Biodiesel in the United States">Feedstock Development program</a> is addressing production of algae on the horizon to expand available material for biodiesel in a sustainable manner.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_policies">International policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: International policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Numerous policies have been put in place since the 1975 oil crisis in order to promote the use of Renewable Fuels in the United States, Canada and Europe. In Canada, these included the implementation of excise taxes exempting propane and natural gas which was extended to ethanol made from biomass and methanol in 1992. The federal government also announced their renewable fuels strategy in 2006 which proposed four components: increasing availability of renewable fuels through regulation, supporting the expansion of Canadian production of renewable fuels, assisting farmers to seize new opportunities in this sector and accelerating the commercialization of new technologies. These mandates were quickly followed by the Canadian provinces:</p>
<p>BC introduced a 5% ethanol and 5% renewable diesel requirement which was effective by January 2010. It also introduced a low carbon fuel requirement for 2012 to 2020.</p>
<p>Alberta introduced a 5% ethanol and 2% renewable diesel requirement implemented April 2011. The province also introduced a minimum 25% GHG emission reduction requirement for qualifying renewable fuels.</p>
<p>Saskatchewan implemented a 2% renewable diesel requirement in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additionally, in 2006, the Canadian Federal Government announced its commitment to using its purchasing power to encourage the biofuel industry. Section three of the 2006 alternative fuels act stated that when it is economically feasible to do so-75% per cent of all federal bodies and crown corporation will be motor vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-Wood_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Research_Council_(Canada)" title="National Research Council (Canada)">National Research Council of Canada</a> has established research on Algal Carbon Conversion as one of its flagship programs.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> As part of this program, the NRC made an announcement in May 2013 that they are partnering with Canadian Natural Resources Limited and Pond Biofuels to construct a demonstration-scale algal biorefinery near Bonnyville, Alberta.<sup id="cite_ref-nrc-cnrc1_172-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrc-cnrc1-172">[172]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States_2">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=46" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Policies in the United States have included a decrease in the subsidies provided by the federal and state governments to the oil industry which have usually included $2.84 billion. This is more than what is actually set aside for the biofuel industry. The measure was discussed at the G20 in Pittsburgh where leaders agreed that "inefficient fossil fuel subsidies encourage wasteful consumption, reduce our energy security, impede investment in clean sources and undermine efforts to deal with the threat of climate change". If this commitment is followed through and subsidies are removed, a fairer market in which algae biofuels can compete will be created. In 2010, the U.S. House of Representatives passed a legislation seeking to give algae-based biofuels parity with cellulose biofuels in federal tax credit programs. The algae-based renewable fuel promotion act (HR 4168) was implemented to give biofuel projects access to a $1.01 per gal production tax credit and 50% bonus depreciation for biofuel plant property. The U.S Government also introduced the domestic Fuel for Enhancing National Security Act implemented in 2011. This policy constitutes an amendment to the Federal property and administrative services act of 1949 and federal defense provisions in order to extend to 15 the number of years that the Department of Defense (DOD) multiyear contract may be entered into the case of the purchase of advanced biofuel. Federal and DOD programs are usually limited to a 5-year period<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_2">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=47" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The European Union (EU) has also responded by quadrupling the credits for second-generation algae biofuels which was established as an amendment to the Biofuels and Fuel Quality Directives<sup id="cite_ref-biofuelstp.eu_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-biofuelstp.eu-188">[188]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Companies">Companies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=48" title="Edit section: Companies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_algal_fuel_producers" title="List of algal fuel producers">List of algal fuel producers</a></div>
<p>With algal biofuel being a relatively new alternative to conventional petroleum products, it leaves numerous opportunities for drastic advances in all aspects of the technology. Producing algae biofuel is not yet a cost-effective replacement for gasoline, but alterations to current methodologies can change this. The two most common targets for advancements are the growth medium (open pond vs. photobioreactor) and methods to remove the intracellular components of the algae. Below are companies that are currently innovating algal biofuel technologies.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Algenol_Biofuels">Algenol Biofuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=49" title="Edit section: Algenol Biofuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Founded in 2006, Algenol Biofuels is a global, industrial biotechnology company that is commercializing its patented algae technology for production of ethanol and other fuels. Based in Southwest Florida, Algenol’s patented technology enables the production of the four most important fuels (ethanol, gasoline, jet, and diesel fuel) using proprietary algae, sunlight, carbon dioxide and saltwater for around $1.27 per gallon and at production levels of 8,000 total gallons of liquid fuel per acre per year. Algenol's technology produces high yields and relies on patented photobioreactors and proprietary downstream techniques for low-cost fuel production using carbon dioxide from industrial sources.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> The company originally intended on producing commercially by 2014, but was set back when Florida Governor Rick Scott signed a bill in 2013 eliminating the state's mandate of a minimum of 10% ethanol in commercial gasoline.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> This caused Algenol CEO Paul Woods to scrap a plan for a US $500 million plant to produce commercial amounts of algae biofuels and pursue other job sites. Currently, Algenol is a partner of the US Department of Energy's Bioenergy Technologies Office, and in 2015 began smaller-scale commercial sales of E15 and E85 ethanol blends to Protec Fuel, a Florida-based fuel distributor.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blue_Marble_Production">Blue Marble Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=50" title="Edit section: Blue Marble Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Blue Marble Production is a Seattle-based company that is dedicated to removing algae from algae-infested water. This in turn cleans up the environment and allows this company to produce biofuel. Rather than just focusing on the mass production of algae, this company focuses on what to do with the byproducts. This company recycles almost 100% of its water via reverse osmosis, saving about 26,000 gallons of water every month. This water is then pumped back into their system. The gas produced as a byproduct of algae will also be recycled by being placed into a photobioreactor system that holds multiple strains of algae. Whatever gas remains is then made into pyrolysis oil by thermochemical processes. Not only does this company seek to produce biofuel, but it also wishes to use algae for a variety of other purposes such as fertilizer, food flavoring, anti-inflammatory, and anti-cancer drugs.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solazyme">Solazyme</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=51" title="Edit section: Solazyme">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Solazyme is one of a handful of companies which is supported by oil companies such as Chevron. Additionally, this company is also backed by Imperium Renewables, Blue Crest Capital Finance, and The Roda Group. Solazyme has developed a way to use up to 80% percent of dry algae as oil.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> This process requires the algae to grow in a dark fermentation vessel and be fed by carbon substrates within their growth media. The effect is the production of triglycerides that are almost identical to vegetable oil. Solazyme's production method is said to produce more oil than those algae cultivated photosynthetically or made to produce ethanol. Oil refineries can then take this algal oil and turn it into biodiesel, renewable diesel or jet fuels.</p>
<p>Part of Solazyme's testing, in collaboration with Maersk Line and the US Navy, placed 30 tons of Soladiesel(RD) algae fuel into the 98,000-tonne, 300-meter container ship Maersk Kalmar. This fuel was used at blends from 7% to 100% in an auxiliary engine on a month-long trip from Bremerhaven, Germany to Pipavav, India in Dec 2011. In Jul 2012, The US Navy used 700,000 gallons of HRD76 biodiesel in three ships of the USS Nimitz "Green Strike Group" during the 2012 RIMPAC exercise in Hawaii. The Nimitz also used 200,000 gallons of HRJ5 jet biofuel. The 50/50 biofuel blends were provided by Solazyme and Dynamic Fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sapphire_Energy">Sapphire Energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=52" title="Edit section: Sapphire Energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sapphire_Energy" title="Sapphire Energy">Sapphire Energy</a> is a leader in the algal biofuel industry backed by the Wellcome Trust, Bill Gates' Cascade Investment, Monsanto, and other large donors.<sup id="cite_ref-sapphireenergy1_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sapphireenergy1-201">[201]</a></sup> After experimenting with production of various algae fuels beginning in 2007, the company now focuses on producing what it calls "green crude" from algae in open raceway ponds. After receiving more than $100 million in federal funds in 2012, Sapphire built the first commercial demonstration algae fuel facility in New Mexico and has continuously produced biofuel since completion of the facility in that year.<sup id="cite_ref-sapphireenergy1_201-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sapphireenergy1-201">[201]</a></sup> In 2013, Sapphire began commercial sales of algal biofuel to <a href="/wiki/Tesoro" title="Tesoro">Tesoro</a>, making it one of the first companies, along with Solazyme, to sell algae fuel on the market.<sup id="cite_ref-Andrew_Herndon_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Andrew_Herndon-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diversified_Technologies_Inc.">Diversified Technologies Inc.</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=53" title="Edit section: Diversified Technologies Inc.">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Diversified Technologies Inc. has created a patent pending pre-treatment option to reduce costs of oil extraction from algae. This technology, called Pulsed Electric Field (PEF) technology, is a low cost, low energy process that applies high voltage electric pulses to a slurry of algae.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> The electric pulses enable the algal cell walls to be ruptured easily, increasing the availability of all cell contents (Lipids, proteins and carbohydrates), allowing the separation into specific components downstream. This alternative method to intracellular extraction has shown the capability to be both integrated in-line as well as scalable into high yield assemblies. The Pulse Electric Field subjects the algae to short, intense bursts of electromagnetic radiation in a treatment chamber, electroporating the cell walls. The formation of holes in the cell wall allows the contents within to flow into the surrounding solution for further separation. PEF technology only requires 1-10 microsecond pulses, enabling a high-throughput approach to algal extraction.</p>
<p>Preliminary calculations have shown that utilization of PEF technology would only account for $0.10 per gallon of algae derived biofuel produced. In comparison, conventional drying and solvent-based extractions account for $1.75 per gallon. This inconsistency between costs can be attributed to the fact that algal drying generally accounts for 75% of the extraction process.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup> Although a relatively new technology, PEF has been successfully used in both food decomtamination processes as well as waste water treatments.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_Oils_Inc.">Origin Oils Inc.</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=54" title="Edit section: Origin Oils Inc.">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Origin Oils Inc. has been researching a revolutionary method called the Helix Bioreactor,<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> altering the common closed-loop growth system. This system utilizes low energy lights in a helical pattern, enabling each algal cell to obtain the required amount of light.<sup id="cite_ref-aquaticbiofuel.com_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aquaticbiofuel.com-206">[206]</a></sup> Sunlight can only penetrate a few inches through algal cells, making light a limiting reagent in open-pond algae farms. Each lighting element in the bioreactor is specially altered to emit specific wavelengths of light, as a full spectrum of light is not beneficial to algae growth. In fact, ultraviolet irradiation is actually detrimental as it inhibits photosynthesis, photoreduction, and the 520&#160;nm light-dark absorbance change of algae.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<p>This bioreactor also addresses another key issue in algal cell growth; introducing CO<sub>2</sub> and nutrients to the algae without disrupting or over-aerating the algae. Origin Oils Inc. combats this issues through the creation of their Quantum Fracturing technology. This process takes the CO<sub>2</sub> and other nutrients, fractures them at extremely high pressures and then deliver the micron sized bubbles to the algae. This allows the nutrients to be delivered at a much lower pressure, maintaining the integrity of the cells.<sup id="cite_ref-aquaticbiofuel.com_206-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aquaticbiofuel.com-206">[206]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proviron">Proviron</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=55" title="Edit section: Proviron">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Proviron is a Belgian microalgae company that also operates in the United States. The company has been working on a new type of reactor (using flat plates) which reduces the cost of algae cultivation. At <a href="/wiki/AlgaePARC" title="AlgaePARC">AlgaePARC</a> similar research is being conducted using 4 grow systems (1 open pond system and 3 types of closed systems). According to René Wijffels the current systems do not yet allow algae fuel to be produced competitively. However using new (closed) systems, and by scaling up the production it would be possible to reduce costs by 10X, up to a price of 0,4 € per kg of algae.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> Currently, Proviron focuses primarily on alternative uses of algae cultures, such as environmentally-conscious plastics, esterification processes, and de-icing processes.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genifuels">Genifuels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=56" title="Edit section: Genifuels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Genifuel Corporation has licensed the high temperature/pressure fuel extraction process and has been working with the team at the lab since 2008. The company intends to team with some industrial partners to create a pilot plant using this process to make biofuel in industrial quantities.<sup id="cite_ref-giz1312_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giz1312-102">[102]</a></sup> Genifuel process combines hydrothermal liquefaction with catalytic hydrothermal gasification in reactor running at 350 Celsius (662 Fahrenheit) and pressure of 3000 PSI.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Qeshm_Microalgae_Biorefinery_Co._.28QMAB.29">Qeshm Microalgae Biorefinery Co. (QMAB)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=57" title="Edit section: Qeshm Microalgae Biorefinery Co. (QMAB)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>QMAB is an Iran-based biofuels company operating solely on the island of Iranian island of Qeshm in the Strait of Hormuz. QMAB's original pilot plant has been operating since 2009, and has a 25,000 Liter capacity.<sup id="cite_ref-algaeindustrymagazine.com_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-algaeindustrymagazine.com-211">[211]</a></sup> In 2014, QMAB released BAYA Biofuel, a biofuel deriving from the algae Nannochloropsis, and has since specified that its unique strain is up to 68% lipids by dry weight volume.<sup id="cite_ref-algaeindustrymagazine.com_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-algaeindustrymagazine.com-211">[211]</a></sup> Development of the farm mainly focuses on 2 phases, production of nutraceutical products and green crude oil to produce biofuel. The main product of their microalgae culture is crude oil, which can be fractioned into the same kinds of fuels and chemical compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae_fuel&amp;action=edit&amp;section=58" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
